Then dim(X/M) = n - m. But we are given that X/M is isomorphic to Y, which has dimension n - k. Therefore, n - m = n - k, which implies that m = k. Therefore, dim(M) = dim(ker(f)), and since M is a subspace of ker(f), they must be equal. Therefore, in finite-dimensional cases, M must be equal to ker(f). But the problem doesn't specify finite-dimensionality. Hmm. So does this hold in infinite dimensions as well? Wait, in infinite-dimensional spaces, dimensions are a bit trickier. But maybe we can still argue based on the isomorphism. If there is an isomorphism between X/M and Y induced by f, then the induced map must be both surjective and injective. Let's think about the induced map. Given f: X → Y is surjective with M ⊂ ker(f), the induced map f': X/M → Y is defined by f'([x]) = f(x). This is well-defined because if [x] = [y], then x - y ∈ M, and since M ⊂ ker(f), f(x - y) = 0, so f(x) = f(y). So f' is well-defined. Now, since f is surjective, f' is also surjective: for any y ∈ Y, there exists x ∈ X such that f(x) = y, hence f'([x]) = y. If f' is an isomorphism, then it must also be injective. The kernel of f' is { [x] | f'(x) = 0 } = { [x] | f(x) = 0 } = { [x] | x ∈ ker(f) }. But in the quotient X/M, the kernel of f' would be ker(f)/M. But since f' is injective, its kernel must be trivial. Therefore, ker(f)/M = {0}, which implies that ker(f) = M. Ah, right! Because if the kernel of f' is trivial, then ker(f)/M must be the zero subspace in X/M, which means that every element of ker(f) is in M. Hence, ker(f) ⊂ M. But we already know M ⊂ ker(f), so equality follows. Therefore, regardless of the dimension, M must equal ker(f). So even in infinite dimensions, this argument holds because we're using the isomorphism directly. Since the induced map f' is injective (as an isomorphism), its kernel must be zero, which forces ker(f) = M. Therefore, the answer should be that M must equal ker(f). So the statement is true; we can't have a proper subspace M of ker(f) such that X/M is isomorphic to Y via the induced map. Let me check if there's a possible counterexample. Suppose someone says, take X = Y ⊕ ker(f), and then take M to be a subspace of ker(f). Then X/M = Y ⊕ (ker(f)/M). But if we have an isomorphism X/M ≅ Y, then ker(f)/M must be zero, hence ker(f) = M. So in this case, you can't have a non-trivial quotient. So even in this case, M must be equal to ker(f). Another angle: Let’s think about the natural projection π: X → X/M. The induced map f': X/M → Y is an isomorphism. Then f = f' ◦ π. Since f' is an isomorphism, it's bijective, so the kernel of f is exactly the kernel of π. But the kernel of π is M. Therefore, ker(f) = M. Wait, that's a succinct way to see it. Since f factors through π via the isomorphism f', the kernel of f must equal the kernel of π, which is M. Hence, ker(f) = M. Therefore, regardless of how you approach it, the conclusion is that M must equal ker(f). So the answer is that M is equal to ker(f), hence the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{M = \ker f} </think> Given two vector spaces \(X\) and \(Y\) with a surjective linear map \(f: X \rightarrow Y\), and a subspace \(M\) of the kernel of \(f\) such that the quotient space \(X/M\) is isomorphic to \(Y\) under the induced map from \(f\), we need to prove or disprove that \(M\) is equal to the kernel of \(f\). 1. **First Isomorphism Theorem**: By the First Isomorphism Theorem, \(X / \ker(f) \cong Y\) because \(f\) is surjective. This implies \(\dim(X / \ker(f)) = \dim(Y)\). 2. **Induced Map**: The induced map \(f': X/M \rightarrow Y\) is well-defined and surjective since \(f\) is surjective. Given \(X/M \cong Y\), \(f'\) must also be injective. 3. **Kernel of the Induced Map**: The kernel of \(f'\) consists of equivalence classes \([x]\) such that \(f(x) = 0\). This kernel is \(\ker(f)/M\). Since \(f'\) is injective, its kernel must be trivial, implying \(\ker(f)/M = \{0\}\). 4. **Equality of Subspaces**: From \(\ker(f)/M = \{0\}\), it follows that every element of \(\ker(f)\) is in \(M\). Since \(M \subset \ker(f)\) by hypothesis, we conclude \(M = \ker(f)\). Thus, the subspace \(M\) must be equal to the kernel of \(f\). \[ \boxed{M = \ker f} \]

Problem: Given two vector spaces $X$ and $Y$, suppose there exists a surjective map $f: X \rightarrow Y$. If $M$ is a subspace of the kernel of $f$ ($M \subset \ker f$) and the quotient space $X/M$ is isomorphic to $Y$ under the induced map from $f$, prove or disprove that $M$ is equal to the kernel of $f$ ($M = \ker f$). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I have this problem here about vector spaces and quotient spaces. Let me try to parse it step by step. First, we have two vector spaces, X and Y. The problem is asking if M must then be equal to ker(f). Let me think. Suppose M is a proper subspace of ker(f). Then the quotient X/M would be a larger space than X/ker(f), right? Because quotienting by a smaller subspace gives a larger quotient space. Wait, no. Wait, actually, if M is contained in ker(f), then the quotient X/M has equivalence classes that are coarser than those in X/ker(f). Wait, maybe not. Let me recall how quotient spaces work. If you have a subspace N contained in another subspace K of X, then X/K is a quotient of X/N. Specifically, there's a natural surjective map from X/N to X/K. So if M is contained in ker(f), then X/M maps onto X/ker(f). But since X/ker(f) is isomorphic to Y, and X/M is also isomorphic to Y, that would require that the surjective map from X/M to X/ker(f) is an isomorphism. But a surjective linear map between vector spaces of the same dimension is an isomorphism. So if X/M and X/ker(f) have the same dimension, then the natural projection from X/M to X/ker(f) is an isomorphism. But when does this happen? Let's put dimensions into this. Suppose X is finite-dimensional for simplicity. Let dim(X) = n, dim(ker(f)) = k, so dim(Y) = n - k by the First Isomorphism Theorem. Now, if M is a subspace of ker(f), then dim(M) = m ≤ k. Then dim(X/M) = n - m. But we are given that X/M is isomorphic to Y, which has dimension n - k. Therefore, n - m = n - k, which implies that m = k. Therefore, dim(M) = dim(ker(f)), and since M is a subspace of ker(f), they must be equal. Therefore, in finite-dimensional cases, M must be equal to ker(f). But the problem doesn't specify finite-dimensionality. Hmm. So does this hold in infinite dimensions as well? Wait, in infinite-dimensional spaces, dimensions are a bit trickier. But maybe we can still argue based on the isomorphism. If there is an isomorphism between X/M and Y induced by f, then the induced map must be both surjective and injective. Let's think about the induced map. Given f: X → Y is surjective with M ⊂ ker(f), the induced map f': X/M → Y is defined by f'([x]) = f(x). This is well-defined because if [x] = [y], then x - y ∈ M, and since M ⊂ ker(f), f(x - y) = 0, so f(x) = f(y). So f' is well-defined. Now, since f is surjective, f' is also surjective: for any y ∈ Y, there exists x ∈ X such that f(x) = y, hence f'([x]) = y. If f' is an isomorphism, then it must also be injective. The kernel of f' is { [x] | f'(x) = 0 } = { [x] | f(x) = 0 } = { [x] | x ∈ ker(f) }. But in the quotient X/M, the kernel of f' would be ker(f)/M. But since f' is injective, its kernel must be trivial. Therefore, ker(f)/M = {0}, which implies that ker(f) = M. Ah, right! Because if the kernel of f' is trivial, then ker(f)/M must be the zero subspace in X/M, which means that every element of ker(f) is in M. Hence, ker(f) ⊂ M. But we already know M ⊂ ker(f), so equality follows. Therefore, regardless of the dimension, M must equal ker(f). So even in infinite dimensions, this argument holds because we're using the isomorphism directly. Since the induced map f' is injective (as an isomorphism), its kernel must be zero, which forces ker(f) = M. Therefore, the answer should be that M must equal ker(f). So the statement is true; we can't have a proper subspace M of ker(f) such that X/M is isomorphic to Y via the induced map. Let me check if there's a possible counterexample. Suppose someone says, take X = Y ⊕ ker(f), and then take M to be a subspace of ker(f). Then X/M = Y ⊕ (ker(f)/M). But if we have an isomorphism X/M ≅ Y, then ker(f)/M must be zero, hence ker(f) = M. So in this case, you can't have a non-trivial quotient. So even in this case, M must be equal to ker(f). Another angle: Let’s think about the natural projection π: X → X/M. The induced map f': X/M → Y is an isomorphism. Then f = f' ◦ π. Since f' is an isomorphism, it's bijective, so the kernel of f is exactly the kernel of π. But the kernel of π is M. Therefore, ker(f) = M. Wait, that's a succinct way to see it. Since f factors through π via the isomorphism f', the kernel of f must equal the kernel of π, which is M. Hence, ker(f) = M. Therefore, regardless of how you approach it, the conclusion is that M must equal ker(f). So the answer is that M is equal to ker(f), hence the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{M = \ker f} </think> Given two vector spaces \(X\) and \(Y\) with a surjective linear map \(f: X \rightarrow Y\), and a subspace \(M\) of the kernel of \(f\) such that the quotient space \(X/M\) is isomorphic to \(Y\) under the induced map from \(f\), we need to prove or disprove that \(M\) is equal to the kernel of \(f\). 1. **First Isomorphism Theorem**: By the First Isomorphism Theorem, \(X / \ker(f) \cong Y\) because \(f\) is surjective. This implies \(\dim(X / \ker(f)) = \dim(Y)\). 2. **Induced Map**: The induced map \(f': X/M \rightarrow Y\) is well-defined and surjective since \(f\) is surjective. Given \(X/M \cong Y\), \(f'\) must also be injective. 3. **Kernel of the Induced Map**: The kernel of \(f'\) consists of equivalence classes \([x]\) such that \(f(x) = 0\). This kernel is \(\ker(f)/M\). Since \(f'\) is injective, its kernel must be trivial, implying \(\ker(f)/M = \{0\}\). 4. **Equality of Subspaces**: From \(\ker(f)/M = \{0\}\), it follows that every element of \(\ker(f)\) is in \(M\). Since \(M \subset \ker(f)\) by hypothesis, we conclude \(M = \ker(f)\). Thus, the subspace \(M\) must be equal to the kernel of \(f\). \[ \boxed{M = \ker f} \]
